<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_baaefc1c-ae29-4a34-816f-1cbd03b61e3e'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }
  //]]>
</script>

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer4070899897672655492">By the end of the 30th century humanity has the capability to travel the universe, to journey beyond earth and beyond the confines of the vulnerable human frame.<br/><br/>The descendants of centuries of scientific, cultural and physical development divide into three: fleshers — true Homo sapiens; Gleisner robots — embodying human minds within machines that interact with the physical</span>
  <span id="freeText4070899897672655492" style="display:none">By the end of the 30th century humanity has the capability to travel the universe, to journey beyond earth and beyond the confines of the vulnerable human frame.<br /><br />The descendants of centuries of scientific, cultural and physical development divide into three: fleshers — true Homo sapiens; Gleisner robots — embodying human minds within machines that interact with the physical world; and polises — supercomputers teeming with intelligent software, containing the direct copies of billions of human personalities now existing only in the virtual reality of the polis.<br /><br />Diaspora is the story of Yatima — a polis being created from random mutations of the Konishi polis base mind seed — and of humankind, Of an astrophysical accident that spurs the thousandfold cloning of the polises. Of the discovery of an alien race and of a kink in time that means humanity — whatever form it takes — will never again be threatened by acts of God.</span>

<div class='communityQuestionSubTitle'>
Popular Answered Questions

<div id='communityQuestionsWithAnswers'>
<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/675951-how-do-distant-clones-solve-the">How do distant clones solve the simultaneity problem of SR? For example, in Chapter 11, &quot;Elena had chose not to wake if any other versions of her had already encountered life&quot;. But the clones are light years away from each other! By the time messages arrive from another clone it could be too late. I find this part really unsatisfatory given the overall rigorous and technically detailed style of the book :-(</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_675951'><a id="like_count_community_question_675951" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/675951?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">2 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F156785.Diaspora">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/675951-how-do-distant-clones-solve-the">4 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/675951-how-do-distant-clones-solve-the">See all 3 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/32944556-alanhk"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_100x100-259587f1619f5253426a4fa6fb508831.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/32944556-alanhk">Alanhk</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">The different ships will arrive at their destinations usually many years apart. So if one does find life, the rest will hear about it in a few years, <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">The different ships will arrive at their destinations usually many years apart. So if one does find life, the rest will hear about it in a few years, while they are still en route, or long after they have arrived. Very unlikely two would make discoveries close enough to make this a problem. (This isn't a SR problem though. Just a "messages take years" problem.)<br /><br />I thought that the citizens finding the prediction of the galactic gamma burst just 1000 years in the future, from a message left a billion years earlier, was a hugely unlikely coincidence. Just near enough to create tension, far enough that they have time to do something. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/941914-am-i-simple-or-something-i-read">Am I simple or something? I read Permutation City and was absolutely blow away, this one though I can not put anything together. I&#39;m 30% in and have ZERO clue of what&#39;s going on. Certainly this book might be written for the mathematically talented.......anyone?</a>

<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/20647308-joe-dean"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1369364470i/20647308._UX100_CR0,6,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/20647308-joe-dean">Joe Dean</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Yes it is confusing at first. it starts in the present on the spaceship (well, present in the future) and then most is a flashback that returns to the<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Yes it is confusing at first. it starts in the present on the spaceship (well, present in the future) and then most is a flashback that returns to the present intermittently. Yeah I'm not mathematically inclined, but I enjoyed trying to visualize the geometry described. I've read three of his books, and this is my favorite. In fact it sort of ruined Sci-Fi for me because it's so good. I used to be an avid reader of Sci-Fi.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2767600060" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2767600060" />
    <a title="BlackOxford" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford"><img alt="BlackOxford" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484863606p2/17744555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2767600060?book_show_action=true">Mar 31, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BlackOxford" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BlackOxford" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford">BlackOxford</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780061052811" href="/book/show/1151990.Diaspora">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=australian">australian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2767600060" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12579065481853115814"><b>The Revised Book of Genesis</b><br/><br/>As is usual with everything by Egan, <i>Diaspora</i> is so densely packed with ideas that all summaries are inadequate. Only one comparison seems even remotely appropriate - to the biblical Book of Genesis.<br/><br/><i>Diaspora</i> is a history of the re-creation of the universe, one in which there is no need for divine power to either start it off or continue its development. In fact, this is a history of how the defects and design flaws of the original creation story are corrected by hard e</span>
  <span id="freeText12579065481853115814" style="display:none"><b>The Revised Book of Genesis</b><br /><br />As is usual with everything by Egan, <i>Diaspora</i> is so densely packed with ideas that all summaries are inadequate. Only one comparison seems even remotely appropriate - to the biblical Book of Genesis.<br /><br /><i>Diaspora</i> is a history of the re-creation of the universe, one in which there is no need for divine power to either start it off or continue its development. In fact, this is a history of how the defects and design flaws of the original creation story are corrected by hard experience. One of those flaws, perhaps the most destructive, is the vulnerability of the universe to arbitrary and uninformed divine intervention.<br /><br />Egan lays out a blueprint for how a very different form of life than has been previously known comes into existence. Moving from the self-referential algorithms necessary to produce consciousness, to the algorithms of transformational topology necessary to impose that consciousness on the world coherently, he provides step by step instructions for the evolution of primitive pyschoblasts, embryonic minds as pure bits of energy, into citizens of the <i>polis</i>, an entirely electronic civilisation.<br /><br />Such citizens are free to explore the Truth Mines containing not just the records of human experience, but the mathematical implications of that experience ‘experienced’ by its fellow-citizens. Through collective reasoning the polis has recognised that reliance on pure deduction is dangerous. It can lead to disaster, particularly that of exponential growth, as previous generations of the original creation had discovered on numerous occasions.<br /><br />In other words there are limits to strictly mathematical reasoning: <i>“The only way to grasp a mathematical concept was to see it in a multitude of different contexts, think through dozens of specific examples, and find at least two or three metaphors to power intuitive speculations... Understanding an idea meant entangling it so thoroughly with all the other symbols in your mind that it changed the way you thought about everything.”</i> This is the function of art. Not just as a means of tempering mathematical logic, but as a way to find undiscovered truths - about ourselves as well as the rest of the universe.<br /><br />The new creation of the <i>polis</i> has also learned how to interact with the old creation of flesh and blood. It can embody itself in robotic form and engage in productive discussion and planning with those unfortunate beings, us, who are the result of the original botched creation-attempt. Although Egan doesn’t claim it explicitly, this is functionally a new religion, one of the self-creation of the world and our total responsibility for it. A remarkable new theology generated from primordial circuitry.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12579065481853115814" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1720190385" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1720190385" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1720190385?book_show_action=true">Aug 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1720190385" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8042092964969242807"><i>“Yatima surveyed the Doppler-shifted stars around the polis, following the frozen, concentric waves of colour across the sky from expansion to convergence. Ve wondered what account they should give of themselves when they finally caught up with their quarry. They’d brought no end of questions to ask, but the flow of information couldn’t all be one-way. When the Transmuters demanded to know ‘Why have you followed us? Why have you come so far?’, where should ve begin?”</i><br/><br/>Where indeed? Initially, the </span>
  <span id="freeText8042092964969242807" style="display:none"><i>“Yatima surveyed the Doppler-shifted stars around the polis, following the frozen, concentric waves of colour across the sky from expansion to convergence. Ve wondered what account they should give of themselves when they finally caught up with their quarry. They’d brought no end of questions to ask, but the flow of information couldn’t all be one-way. When the Transmuters demanded to know ‘Why have you followed us? Why have you come so far?’, where should ve begin?”</i><br><br>Where indeed? Initially, the first twenty or so pages of <i>Diaspora</i> made no sense to me, the words seem to have originated from a parallel universe where, presumably, they make perfect sense there. To be honest I briefly considered giving it up even at that early stage. Still, the USD 6.58 I paid for my copy demands that I get every hard-earned penny worth or die trying. So I did something I seldom do, I checked out reviews of this book on Goodreads and other websites, on some of these reviews certain key concepts are clearly explained. After that, I reread those initial ten pages again and this time it makes enough sense to continue. Better still, around page 50 my jaw dropped, and I thought “Wow! Far out!”<br><br><i>Diaspora</i> is set in the far future, more than 1000 years from now. Humanity have evolved or developed into three strands. The “fleshers” are biological transhumans, including modified humans (people with gills, wings, and whatnot), the “gleisner robots” are human minds, digitalized and residing in machines, and the “citizens”, originally these were humans who have uploaded their consciousness into computers as sentient software, successive generations were entirely created inside computers. After an unexpected cosmic disaster kills off billions of fleshers the gleisner robots and the citizens go out into space to search for a way to save humanity from future disasters of this kind (the fleshers guys are not really in a position to contribute).<br><br>While the narrative switches to a few point of view characters, one particular character can be singled out as the protagonist, a citizen called Yatima. The novel starts with ver* frame narrative then the author depicts vis* beginning as a kind of raw data strand, then the OS develops her into a fully sentient being in software form. The stages of ver development from raw data to personhood is explained in detail by Egan, I don't understand most of the science but enough of it makes sense to blow my mind. Greg Egan really puts the “hard” back into hard science fiction.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1471047374i/19995406._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>A more representative book cover<br><br>His brand of sci-fi is extremely clever but often not accessible to those of us who do not have a strong grounding in several branches of science, particularly biochemistry, physics, astrophysics, and computer science. That said, I don't want to dissuade anyone from reading his books, he is a very capable storyteller, his plot can generally be followed even if you don't understand some of the scientific basis of the story. His characters are mostly quite well written, in spite of many of them not being quite human. By the middle of the book, I was expecting to rate diaspora five stars and recommend that everybody in the world should read it. Unfortunately, Greg Egan's advanced scientific and mathematical expositions soon begin to overwhelm me and the narrative becomes harder and harder to follow. Here is an example passage:<br><br><i>“The macrosphere’s four-dimensional standard fibre yielded a much smaller set of fundamental particles than the ordinary universe’s six-dimensional one. In place of six flavours of quarks and six flavours of leptons there was just one of each, plus their antiparticles. There were gluons, gravitons and photons, but no W or Z bosons, since they mediated the process of quarks changing flavour. Three quarks or three antiquarks together formed a charged ‘nucleon’ or ‘anti-nucleon’, similar to an ordinary proton or antiproton, and the sole lepton and its antiparticle were much like an electron and positron, but there was no combination of quarks analogous to a neutron.”</i><br><br>If the above paragraph makes sense to you don’t even bother with the rest of this review, you don’t need to know what a science ignoramus thinks, just go and grab a copy of <i>Diaspora</i> today. To be honest, I have no idea what that paragraph means. I am sure that if I understood it I would be amazed at his ingenious application of whatever scientific principle he is describing. As it is, whenever Egan goes into his super science mode, my attention starts to drift away from the narrative as it is not anchored to anything I can understand. There are too many passages like that in the second half of the book for me to concentrate on the story. While I greatly admire the book and love at least half of it I was quite relieved when I arrived at the end because by then I was no longer so engrossed by it. So, my scientific ignorance notwithstanding, <i>Diaspora</i> is a work of staggering imagination. <br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1369935544l/17999934.jpg" width="370" height="534" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br>Excellent Hungarian cover<br><br>I do recommend this book with some caveats. For readers with the capability to understand much of the advance science I recommend this book very highly. For less learned persons like myself, I would recommend it with a caution that a substantial part of the book may go over your head but it is still a good mind stretcher, a gymnasium for the imagination. I did feel frustrated with it from time to time but I am still glad I read it. If you never read anything by Greg Egan before I suggest starting with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/156784.Permutation_City__Subjective_Cosmology__2_" title="Permutation City (Subjective Cosmology #2) by Greg Egan" rel="nofollow noopener"> Permutation City</a> which also features characters who are living software. I find <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/156784.Permutation_City__Subjective_Cosmology__2_" title="Permutation City (Subjective Cosmology #2) by Greg Egan" rel="nofollow noopener"> Permutation City</a> to be much more accessible and also mind-expanding.<br>___________________<br><br><b>Notes:</b><br>*Due to lack of gender among most of the citizens, a new set of pronouns are used: ve, ver, and vis. Ve is the subject pronoun used in place of he and she, ver is the object pronoun (instead of him or her), and vis is the possessive pronoun (in place of his and her). A few citizens do choose to have a gender, however, when you are a piece of software you can be anything.<br><br>• It also helps to understand the word “polis”, to help access the early part of the book. Nothing to do with cops, a polis is basically a computer system hosting citizens. There are several polises on Earth, we are only introduced to a few.<br><br>• How would you like to be a "Citizen", a software transhuman? I personally would not mind, the "dev" may be able to debug me, this would probably take a while!<br><br>• A writer at Tor.com asks “<a href="http://www.tor.com/2008/12/18/egansuperstar/" rel="nofollow noopener">Why Isn’t Greg Egan A Superstar?</a>”. I do think he is great, but I am not surprised he is not a “superstar”. His books are much smarter than <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4763.John_Scalzi" title="John Scalzi" rel="nofollow noopener"> John Scalzi</a>’s bestsellers but much more demanding to read and much less commercial. I have a feeling he doesn’t care, he writes what he wants to write, no compromises, kudos to him.<br><br>• Star rating: I think objectively it is worth 5 stars, subjectively, in term of accessibility to me I would rate it at: <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1434376590i/15212605._SX540_.png" width="150" height="28" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br>___________________<br><br><b>Quotes:</b><br><i>“Paolo assimilated all the stored transmissions from his Earth-self – almost three hundred years’ worth – before composing a reply.”</i><br>Cloning versions of yourself is easy when you are a software person.<br><br><i> “Fleshers used to spin fantasies about aliens arriving to “conquer” Earth, to steal their “precious” physical resources, to wipe them out for fear of “competition” . . . as if a species capable of making the journey wouldn’t have had the power, or the wit, or the imagination, to rid itself of obsolete biological imperatives. Conquering the galaxy is what bacteria with spaceships would do – knowing no better, having no choice.”</i><br>I love this passage. Very sage.<br><br><i>“He’d found Blanca’s rigorous six-dimensional space-time curvature treatment heavy going, and he’d skimmed over the hard parts where the Einstein tensor equation was derived by approximating the interactions between massive particles and virtual gravitons.” </i><br>Even one of the characters finds some of the science-heavy going!<br><br><i>“At both ends of the linear accelerator, electron-positron pairs were created in small cyclotrons; the positrons were retained in storage rings, while the electrons were fed straight into the main accelerator. The opposing beams met in the centre of the Forge, and if two electrons collided head-on, fast enough to overcome electrostatic repulsion, Kozuch Theory predicted that they’d splice wormholes. </i><br>No idea what this means, apart from splicing wormholes bit.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8042092964969242807" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_311068942" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/311068942" />
    <a title="Aaron Arnold" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3749410-aaron-arnold"><img alt="Aaron Arnold" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1525666494p2/3749410.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/311068942?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aaron Arnold" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aaron Arnold" href="/user/show/3749410-aaron-arnold">Aaron Arnold</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780752809250" href="/book/show/1281535.Diaspora">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3749410-aaron-arnold?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3749410-aaron-arnold?shelf=read-in-2010">read-in-2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3749410-aaron-arnold?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer311068942" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1965621332086497559">Ever since I read Permutation City, Egan has been one of my favorite hard sci-fi authors, and when I cracked open this book and saw that the first forty pages were a hardcore blow-by-blow of an AI becoming self- aware that would do Marvin Minsky proud, I knew that I would love it too. Brief plot synopsis: in the near future where humanity has trifurcated into AIs, sentient robots, and flesh-bound transhumans, an unexplained binary neutron star collision and subsequent gamma ray burst forces the </span>
  <span id="freeText1965621332086497559" style="display:none">Ever since I read Permutation City, Egan has been one of my favorite hard sci-fi authors, and when I cracked open this book and saw that the first forty pages were a hardcore blow-by-blow of an AI becoming self- aware that would do Marvin Minsky proud, I knew that I would love it too. Brief plot synopsis: in the near future where humanity has trifurcated into AIs, sentient robots, and flesh-bound transhumans, an unexplained binary neutron star collision and subsequent gamma ray burst forces the remnants of civilization to colonize the galaxy in order to prevent such an extinction event from ever happening again. While Diaspora is of course filled with laugh-out-loud clumsy infodumps ("Say, can you tell me about your trans-universal particle physics model?" "Not until I brief you on hyperdimensional topology!"), it was also a surprisingly strong exploration of how humans – no matter if they're flesh and blood or sentient programs – cope with death and loss. I suppose that this is a common theme in Egan's works, and some of the AI characters do seem somewhat reprised from Permutation City, but Diaspora connects the deaths of individuals to the extinction of human life in a clever and meaningful way, and also ties that into personal and societal maturity (what happens when you want to simply stop exploring?) in a way that reminded me of Stanislaw Lem's Cyberiad, which is high praise. Science fiction at its best uses both existing and imaginary technology to explore old human issues in new ways, and it's a shame Egan isn't more famous because he does that as well as anyone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1965621332086497559" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_374496797" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/374496797" />
    <a title="Kara Babcock" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1188122-kara-babcock"><img alt="Kara Babcock" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580776593p2/1188122.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/374496797?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kara Babcock" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kara Babcock" href="/user/show/1188122-kara-babcock">Kara Babcock</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780752809250" href="/book/show/1281535.Diaspora">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=2012-read">2012-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=posthuman">posthuman</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=didactic-fiction">didactic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=post-apocalyptic">post-apocalyptic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer374496797" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10809572361979428774">I want to give this book five stars. I want to give this book one star. It’s amazing. It’s terrible.<br/><br/>Keeping Earth habitable is a pressing concern today. Even if we manage to avoid eco-catastrophe (and I’m optimistic on this), that’s only a small hurdle in the grand scheme of the cosmos. We only have about a billion years left before the Sun swells so much that it cooks the atmosphere. A few billion years after that, the Sun will engulf Earth itself—bye, bye, homeworld. Even if we manage to emigr</span>
  <span id="freeText10809572361979428774" style="display:none">I want to give this book five stars. I want to give this book one star. It’s amazing. It’s terrible.<br><br>Keeping Earth habitable is a pressing concern today. Even if we manage to avoid eco-catastrophe (and I’m optimistic on this), that’s only a small hurdle in the grand scheme of the cosmos. We only have about a billion years left before the Sun swells so much that it cooks the atmosphere. A few billion years after that, the Sun will engulf Earth itself—bye, bye, homeworld. Even if we manage to emigrate to the outer solar system or other solar systems entirely, we’re still just buying time until the end of the universe—whether it’s heat death or a Big Crunch or something else entirely. We can’t outrun eternity.<br><br>Of course, if we are around in a billion, let alone ten billion years, I somehow doubt we would recognize our future selves. Considering how much we differ from our hominin ancestors a million years ago, I suspect that evolution—natural or artificially-induced—will carry us away from this body plan. If we are going to migrate, we will adapt our forms—in body, or just in mind. <em>Diaspora</em> is a vision of a possible future, one in which humanity has diversified itself, speciated itself, and it attempting to find a way to survive.<br><br><em>Diaspora</em> is a challenging novel, intentionally so. It is no-holds-barred, no-punches-pulled posthumanity. By the thirtieth century, humanity exists in three forms: flesh humans on Earth, embodied robots floating around the outer solar system, and polises of minds running as software on immense architectures. The main characters are exclusively minds from a polis. After an unanticipated, unexplained gamma-ray burst irradiates Earth, precipitating the extinction of flesh humanity, these minds resolve to explain the phenomenon and find a way of protecting humanity from it in the future. This triggers a strange and wonderful exploration of physical space and theoretical physics, and more mathematical exposition than you can shake a stick at.<br><br>The bulk of this book is a discussion of high-level mathematics and highly theoretical, even speculative, physics. Everything from high-energy wormholes to alternative universe topologies makes an appearance here. As a mathematician, and one who loves the abstract, axiomatic fields, I enjoyed most of this. It’s nice to step into someone else’s series of “what if” scenarios. When these are combined with the exploration of the physical universe and encounters with extraterrestrial life, it’s even cooler. The ideas that Egan explores here are intriguing enough to make me want to give <em>Diaspora</em> 5 stars—if it were a blog post, maybe.<br><br>Greg Egan bypasses the conventional structure of a novel, giving us instead mathematical musings in four acts. Technically <em>Diaspora</em> has everything a novel needs: characters, dialogue, even a plot. But with nature as the sole antagonist, the threats in this book are extremely distant. They are existential (though I hesitate to use that word when these characters are cloning themselves thousands of times over), but only in the most distant sense. This is literally a book about the end of the world as we <em>don’t</em> know it, and it’s almost as difficult for me to wrap my head around as the physics and mathematics are.<br><br>I think it would be tempting to seize upon the very abstract subject matter and level the charge that <em>Diaspora</em> is difficult because, with so many posthumans leaving flesh behind for a sixteen-dimensional universe, it loses something of what it means to be human. I understand why some people would feel this way, but I don’t think it’s the case at all. In spite of the very technical and dry dialogue between these characters, Egan makes it clear that their main concern—other than survival—is the preservation of <em>humanity</em>. There are, if not conflicts, then arguments between characters about the best path to take to remain human—the merits of flesh versus software, the perils of solipsism. Indeed, <em>Diaspora</em> is about the ultimate quest to remain human in spite of the universe itself stacking the deck against you.<br><br>I’m not going to give <em>Diaspora</em> five stars, because I think other authors have done this better while still delivering a very compelling <em>story</em>. I’m not going to give <em>Diaspora</em> one star, because it is an amazing collection of ideas and dialogues about humanity, progress, and physics. It’s like a really trippy blog post, just masquerading as a novel because novels, like bow ties, are cool. And like anything pretending to be something it’s not, <em>Diaspora</em> isn’t quite as satisfying as the conventional novel we’ve been trained to enjoy. It’s not bad, but different, and anything too different has to work a lot harder to earn acclaim. I’m willing to meet it halfway, so I’ll give it a solid three stars.<br><br>I majored in math and minored in English and philosophy; I’m teaching math <em>and</em> English to high school students come this fall. The intersectionality of this novel is kind of tailor-made for me—I suspect other people might have a hard time with it, and I want to be very upfront with this opinion lest my enthusiasm for the subject lead you astray. But <em>if</em> you are willing to make the effort and tolerate the paper-thin plot, then … <em>wow</em>. Yeah, in a way, totally worth it.<br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="Creative Commons BY-NC License" width="88" height="31" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380238653i/681550.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10809572361979428774" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2021658258" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2021658258" />
    <a title="Erik" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1639329-erik"><img alt="Erik" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1440429204p2/1639329.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2021658258?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erik" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erik" href="/user/show/1639329-erik">Erik</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1639329-erik?shelf=top-shelf">top-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1639329-erik?shelf=scififantasy">scififantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1639329-erik?shelf=detailed-review">detailed-review</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2021658258" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7282973720560905298">Diaspora is one of the greatest science fiction books I have ever read. Reading it brought into my mind a sense of wonder and of sheer visceral infinity that I hadn’t felt for years.<br/><br/>And yet I would recommend this ambitious hard sci-fi novel to almost no one. How can that be? How does such a strange, lonely situation arise?<br/><br/>Cue digression:<br/><br/>Have you ever seen a Shakespeare play? I mean an actual play, performed live on stage, in the original early English.<br/><br/>The first such play I saw was the Duchess of</span>
  <span id="freeText7282973720560905298" style="display:none">Diaspora is one of the greatest science fiction books I have ever read. Reading it brought into my mind a sense of wonder and of sheer visceral infinity that I hadn’t felt for years.<br><br>And yet I would recommend this ambitious hard sci-fi novel to almost no one. How can that be? How does such a strange, lonely situation arise?<br><br>Cue digression:<br><br>Have you ever seen a Shakespeare play? I mean an actual play, performed live on stage, in the original early English.<br><br>The first such play I saw was the Duchess of Malfi, written by Shakespeare’s contemporary John Webster and if you’ve never partaken of something similar, allow me to explain:<br><br>It is practically impossible to consciously track and interpret every line uttered in a play written in iambic pentameter (or some other poetical meter). On top of being in an early dialect, such lines are written for beauty and lyricism as much as they are for meaning and clarity. E.g.:<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>I know death hath ten thousand several doors<br>For men to take their exits; and 'tis found<br>They go on such strange geometrical hinges,<br>You may open them both ways: any way, for heaven-sake.</i>
</blockquote><br>This is in STARK contrast to the media we encounter on the golden and silver screens, in which narration and dialogue must be immediately understandable by even persons of rather lackluster intelligence.<br><br>My thought, as I was watching the Duchess of Malfi, was that most of my students would find it utterly incomprehensible. Their fluency in daily English is passable, I guess, but it is almost purely functional. They get confounded any time I turn my vocab or lyricism up a notch, which I occasionally do out of an incorrigible impishness. These students would hate the play. They’d feel lost. They hadn’t gained the skills (if just because of their youth) necessary to appreciate what was going on there.<br><br>On the other hand, I absolutely *loved* Duchess of Malfi. The experience transcended my highest expectations. It felt, in many ways, like a boon the Universe was offering me, to reward my diligence in studying language and literature.<br><br>Because, sure, it’s difficult to swiftly track the meaning of every single line uttered but you don’t have to. The tropes, the conflicts, the characterizations – the big thematic stuff – are not dissimilar to those in contemporary stories. Realizing that I could apply my *modern* sense of humanity to connect with a story written FIVE centuries ago invoked a most delightful sense of empathy with, well, with dead people, a feeling I will call NECROPATHY. My ancestors were not so different than I. Their concerns were my concerns. Their humor, my humor. Their tragedy, my tragedy.<br><br>Reading Diaspora is similar to watching a Shakespeare play, except the difficulty lies not in the lyricism and age of language but in the book’s mathematical, scientific framing. I don’t just mean technical knowledge of these topics, though that is helpful in interpreting dialogue such as, “This solution [of a wormhole] has positive mass. In fact, if GR [General Relativity] held true at this scale, it would just be a pair of black holes sharing a singularity. Of course, even for the heaviest elementary particles the Schwarzchild radius is far smaller than the Planck-Wheeler length, so quantum uncertainty would disrupt any potential event horizons, and perhaps even smooth away the singularity as well. But I wanted to find a simple, geometrical model underlying the uncertainty.”<br><br>If you have no familiarity with relativity, if you don’t know a Schwarzchild radius from a <i>trous noir</i>, if you mostly know Heisenberg as that guy that cooks really high-quality meth in that TV show, then yeah that sentence is going to be gobbly-de-gook to you.<br><br>But even simple interpretation is not enough. It’s more than just a matter of vocabulary, it’s about understanding the deeper importance, why any of this math, science craziness even MATTERS.<br><br>In fact, I deal with this situation all the time. As a math and science teacher, I constantly must field queries along the line of, “Why do I need to learn this?” or “When will I ever use this in my career?”<br><br>This question excites me tremendously, primarily because it’s not a real question. Students ask it contemptuously, confident there is no real actual valid answer. Indeed, they aren’t actually interested in hearing the answer. What they’re really doing is materializing an excuse for their apathy (and/or low skill) toward what they’re doing. It’s OK if they don’t give a damn about what they’re doing if what they’re doing is unimportant.<br><br>UNFORTUNATELY FOR THEM, they did ask the question, and by asking it, they have voluntarily stepped into the ring with THE MATHLOSSUS.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1497631974ra/23045066.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>FIE! I begin. What are you, some sort of learning mercenary? Do you only ever learn if it makes you money? Oh, how dreary such a life will be.<br><br>Math and science, I continue, are the language of the universe and, indeed, of divinity. Do you think God would deign to speak in English? Spanish? Latin? Greek? Hebrew? Arabic? How parochial! God, insofar as he exists, would speak in the language of the Universe, the Universal Language, which is the language of nature and reality, which our human-created mathematics and physics most closely approximate.<br><br>It is for this reason that never should you listen to any person who does not understand math and science speak about God. How arrogant, how brash for one who knows so little of the universe to presume to speak about the nature of it.<br><br>WELL, Diaspora, while written in English, is a story about the Universe and thus demands one know the language of the Universe.<br><br>In that sense, the book is a like a physics or engineering thriller, which is far more exciting and interesting than it sounds (provided, that is, you speak the language of the Universe). The prime quest of the novel boils down to the protagonists needing to understand the universe in order to survive.<br><br>But if that’s ALL it was, I wouldn’t be calling it the ‘one of the greatest sci-fi books I’ve ever read.’<br><br>Diaspora also offers the best (in terms of insight and realism) exploration of a post-human milieu that I’ve ever read. It begins in the year 2975 (you don’t even want to know what year it ends), at which point humanity – still in the solar system – has split into three factions: fleshers; gleisners; and ‘virtual’ humans living in polises. Fleshers are biological humans, though with advanced gene technology. Gleisners are humans who have opted for robot bodies. And those in the polis are those who have fully embraced becoming software and existing within virtual scapes – which, by the way, operate at 800 times the speed of meat existence. That is, a citizen of the polis experiences 800 years for every one year a flesher does (and yes the book explores the greater ramifications of such time dilation).<br><br>The story primarily focuses on the experience of those in the polis and does an incredible job of exploring just what it means to exist purely as software. In fact, the very first chapter involves a newly born software ‘mind-seed’ growing into self-awareness. It is absolutely stunning. It is a tour de force of genius – and the entire rest of the book is just as creative and intelligent.<br><br>And the scope! The scope of this book is enormous. I’d explain, but I don’t want to spoil, except to say despite being wildly ambitious, it feels authentic throughout. This is a testament to just how skillfully Greg Egan weaves hard sci-fi with more imaginative soft sci-fi.<br><br>So, whew, yeah. Let me summarize. I think you can enjoy this book even without a math and science background. But if you do have such a background, it is *extremely* rewarding. You absolutely owe it to yourself to read this book. It will make every other sci-fi book seem less like sci-fi and more like fantasy.<br><br>***<br><br>Addendum: If you feel intimidated, I extend the following offer: If you read this book and get stuck in the technical details, you should feel free to send me a message here. I will do my best to explain the science and math stuff – particularly its greater significance – to assist in your reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7282973720560905298" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5158571113994298942">If you're into stuff like this, <a href='https://manuelaantao.blogspot.com/2019/05/mobius-strip-diaspora-by-greg-egan.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>you can read the full review.</a><br/><br/><br/><br/>Möbius Strip: "Diaspora" by Greg Egan<br/><br/><br/><br/>Let’s try an experiment.<br/><br/>Make a rectangle of paper with y width and y times 3.14 length with a little excess enough to connect the ends. Twist the rectangle and connect the ends into a Möbius strip. You'll wind up with something that looks like two joined cones and almost a solid. I like to call it a "Möbius mollusk" since it resembles a conical seashell. Like a seashell it has an opening, two in fac</span>
  <span id="freeText5158571113994298942" style="display:none">If you're into stuff like this, <a href="https://manuelaantao.blogspot.com/2019/05/mobius-strip-diaspora-by-greg-egan.html" rel="nofollow noopener">you can read the full review.</a><br /><br /><br /><br />Möbius Strip: "Diaspora" by Greg Egan<br /><br /><br /><br />Let’s try an experiment.<br /><br />Make a rectangle of paper with y width and y times 3.14 length with a little excess enough to connect the ends. Twist the rectangle and connect the ends into a Möbius strip. You'll wind up with something that looks like two joined cones and almost a solid. I like to call it a "Möbius mollusk" since it resembles a conical seashell. Like a seashell it has an opening, two in fact, that I call "ears". Now you can make smaller Möbius mollusks of the original width divided by 1.61 (Phi) with a length again of that new width times 3.14. These smaller Möbius strips will nest recursively into the ear of each previous larger strip.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_1276230029" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1276230029" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1276230029?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2019-shelf">2019-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=space-opera">space-opera</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=transhumanism">transhumanism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=worldbuilding-sf">worldbuilding-sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1276230029" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16439735429522637606">I am very safe in saying that this is one hell of an ambitious, dense, and thoroughly grounded novel of mind-blowing physics housed in one of the most hardcore hard-SF frames I've ever seen.<br/><br/>That's including Cixin Liu's recent trilogy.<br/><br/>I've read a lot of physics books for the sheer pleasure of it and I have a pretty good imagination, but when I was reading this particular novel, I was hard-pressed to keep up with the wall of information, exposition, and detailed descriptions of particle and quantu</span>
  <span id="freeText16439735429522637606" style="display:none">I am very safe in saying that this is one hell of an ambitious, dense, and thoroughly grounded novel of mind-blowing physics housed in one of the most hardcore hard-SF frames I've ever seen.<br /><br />That's including Cixin Liu's recent trilogy.<br /><br />I've read a lot of physics books for the sheer pleasure of it and I have a pretty good imagination, but when I was reading this particular novel, I was hard-pressed to keep up with the wall of information, exposition, and detailed descriptions of particle and quantum physics, theoretical frameworks, then more theoretical frameworks branching off the first, and then yet more in case we might have been getting used to the previously heavy load. :)<br /><br />Am I complaining? No. Hell no. In fact, I'm frankly amazed and thrilled. The underlying story feels like a MORE coherent and theoretical run on Vinge's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77711.A_Fire_Upon_the_Deep__Zones_of_Thought___1_" title="A Fire Upon the Deep (Zones of Thought, #1) by Vernor Vinge" rel="nofollow noopener">A Fire Upon the Deep</a>, delving much deeper into the possibilities brought up by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/61666.Contact" title="Contact by Carl Sagan" rel="nofollow noopener">Contact</a>, and it goes just about as far as you can go in transhumanism, ranging widely between regular humans, purely software/robot humans, and virtual polities within wide-umbrella AIs housing vast numbers of uploaded personalities.<br /><br />The center of the galaxy went boom. It's the end of all life. Run. Run! Run!!!!! :) Vast number of years and high tech isn't enough to escape this. <br /><br />What we have here is a full and vast adventure of exploration, discovery, and a mind-blowing physics reveal that not only lets the reader fall sideways through the universe and multiple dimensions, but it does it in an excellently ACCURATE direction (at least as far as we understand current physics). <br /><br />The added realism is bolstered by a very excellent bibliography at the end and I can attest to the quality of at least three-quarters of them. :)<br /><br />While this novel is NOT all that accessible to casual readers of SF, it IS extremely rewarding to those who are willing to sit through long theoretical (and not so theoretical) modern mind-blowing physics lessons. Is all the science necessary?<br /><br />Hell yes, at least the way the plot requires them. :) This novel will not hand-hold anyone. And for that, my hat goes off. Much, much respect. :)<br /><br />Oh, the novel makes me feel stupid, too. :) But that's okay. I've already sealed away a copy of it in a time capsule that will open in a thousand years for the enjoyment of our machine children with brains made of neutrinos who will have all the underpinning physics written into their bones.<br /><br />:)<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16439735429522637606" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_407276679" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/407276679" />
    <a title="Courtney" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/697731-courtney"><img alt="Courtney" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1383026361p2/697731.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/407276679?book_show_action=true">Sep 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Courtney" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Courtney" href="/user/show/697731-courtney">Courtney</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer407276679" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13037427723986276835">If you come to hard sci-fi in search of ideas about how humanity might change as we integrate with our machines, or how the universe (or universes) might fit together well beyond the observable world we know, you might well love this book. It's got some interesting ideas. Unfortunately, it's so bogged down by over-the-top "science-ish" writing, weak character development and two oddly stitched together plots that I kept cursing in annoyance as I read, rather than delighting at the novelty of aut</span>
  <span id="freeText13037427723986276835" style="display:none">If you come to hard sci-fi in search of ideas about how humanity might change as we integrate with our machines, or how the universe (or universes) might fit together well beyond the observable world we know, you might well love this book. It's got some interesting ideas. Unfortunately, it's so bogged down by over-the-top "science-ish" writing, weak character development and two oddly stitched together plots that I kept cursing in annoyance as I read, rather than delighting at the novelty of author Greg Egan's ideas.<br /><br />Each of the two stitched together plots is based on a distinct premise.<br /><br />The first premise: It's the distant future. People can be male, female or genderless. They can inhabit mortal bodies like our own, or genetically altered bodies, or immortal robot bodies, or they can become converted into immortal human minds/souls that live forever in the digital realm. And some people -- including Yatima -- are born digitally, the result of a DNA-like sequence that allows randomness to shape the ingredients necessary for humanity into a self-aware being. People being what they are, those who inhabit mortal bodies think theirs is the only "true" way to experience humanity, the fully digital souls don't understand why anyone would choose to be limited by the constraints of the physical world, the robot dwellers think theirs is the perfect compromise, all seem to prefer separate spheres of existence as they remain warily aware of the other modes of being.<br /><br />The second premise comes a fair chunk of the way in to the book, after the first premise is firmly established: All these different kinds of folks have just become aware that world-destroying (and maybe even solar-system-destroying or galaxy-destroying) astronomical events are more likely and harder to predict than they've ever understood before. Now the race is on to find a way to escape or hide before humanity and life come to an end. It's not clear if survival requires traveling great distances across the universe, or innovations in science to create wormholes, or the discovery of non-human intelligence, or another path altogether. We follow a group of digital souls as they venture far from earth in search of understanding and salvation.<br /><br />Both premises are intellectually interesting, though they seem kind of disjointed, as does the array of characters we meet along the way. The book starts by introducing us to Yatima, a genderless new-born digital soul, as "ve" explores "ver" world, and introduces us to this world along the way. We get to know and like a number of characters, and it's frustrating when, in the second half of the book, a huge chunk of these characters fade from view completely, or disappear until the last few pages.<br /><br />Egan also doesn't know when to stop with his detailed explanations of the speculative/imagined science that underpins the vast world he's imagined. These explanations read like dense academic treatises, except that they aren't real science, and they bog down the book. As one of these quagmires of over-explanations opens the book, I'm amazed that thousands of readers appear to have been, like me, stubborn enough to push through to see what the story might really be about. As I read, I learned to skim the science-ese and start paying attention at the end of each of these passages. Otherwise, I probably wouldn't have made it through.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13037427723986276835" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_101956190" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101956190" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/155663-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581956036p2/155663.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/101956190?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/155663-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/155663-david?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101956190" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9045131473032334177">I love the super-technical approach in this book. There is a rich combination of hard-core chemistry, biology, particle physics, astronomy, cosmology, mathematics; and that is on top of technologies like super-computing, artificial intelligence and bio-engineering. The first section on the pre-birth development of Yatima is mind-blowing; bio-engineering, psycho-engineering, just a wealth of concepts that left me breathless.<br/><br/>There are three types of "people" in the story. There are regular human b</span>
  <span id="freeText9045131473032334177" style="display:none">I love the super-technical approach in this book. There is a rich combination of hard-core chemistry, biology, particle physics, astronomy, cosmology, mathematics; and that is on top of technologies like super-computing, artificial intelligence and bio-engineering. The first section on the pre-birth development of Yatima is mind-blowing; bio-engineering, psycho-engineering, just a wealth of concepts that left me breathless.<br /><br />There are three types of "people" in the story. There are regular human beings--sort of like Luddites. There are robots that run on artificial intelligence. And there are "polises", that are pure personalities that are run on super-computers. Most of the story is about the polises, who are super-intelligent spirits (?) that drop mind seeds with clones of themselves onto various planets.<br /><br />The drama starts when an astronomer notices that the rotation rate of nearby double neutron star system starts to slow down. The slow-down is much more rapid than could be expected from known physics. When the neutron star system comes to a stop, an intense cosmic ray shower is expected to disrupt life on Earth. <br /><br />And then, things start to get really weird. Infinite dimensions. Infinite universes. I'm not sure exactly what a "character" in the book really is. "People" just aren't what they used to be. And I can't figure out how characters/clones get from one star system to another, from one set of dimensions to another set, or from one universe to the next. As Yogi Berra said, "The future isn't what it used to be."</span>
  <a data-text-id="9045131473032334177" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/333182948?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charles" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charles" href="/user/show/5290504-charles">Charles</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer333182948" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12202486204372577838">Absolutely stunning concepts are fired at you every couple of pages, coupled with a writing style which makes hard science-fiction just about comprehensible. The feeling of the vertigo of extreme knowledge reminds me of Arthur C Clarke and Olaf Stapledon at their unsettlingly cognizant best.<br/><br/>Importantly, it's worth noting that this is a narrative leap forward from it's spiritual predecessor, Permutation City, which tended towards being quite dry, despite it's philosophical enormity. In Diaspora, </span>
  <span id="freeText12202486204372577838" style="display:none">Absolutely stunning concepts are fired at you every couple of pages, coupled with a writing style which makes hard science-fiction just about comprehensible. The feeling of the vertigo of extreme knowledge reminds me of Arthur C Clarke and Olaf Stapledon at their unsettlingly cognizant best.<br /><br />Importantly, it's worth noting that this is a narrative leap forward from it's spiritual predecessor, Permutation City, which tended towards being quite dry, despite it's philosophical enormity. In Diaspora, Egan manages to create a compelling narrative despite the ludicrous timescales and scope existential scope.<br /><br />This is an affecting, thought provoking and energetic treatment of the meaning of life taken to many absolute extremes. Both complete solipsism and limitless personal expansion are investigated, as are the seemingly infinite and arbitrary positions in between. Not since reading Star Maker or The City and the Stars have I been so energised by the possibilities of scope for a narrative.<br /><br />Spectacular.</span>

<div id="review_161721691" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/161721691" />
    <a title="Paul  Perry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4107783-paul-perry"><img alt="Paul  Perry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1606145814p2/4107783.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/161721691?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul  Perry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul  Perry" href="/user/show/4107783-paul-perry">Paul  Perry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780752809250" href="/book/show/1281535.Diaspora">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Paul  by: </span>
      open minds, hard sf fans, science nerds
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=male-author">male-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=sf">sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=hard-sf">hard-sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=future-history">future-history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer161721691" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4252734388141795659"><br/><br/>	My memories of when I used to subscribe to the science fiction magazine Interzone in the 80s and 90s are largely of two types of stories. The magazine had a penchant for a brand of rather gloomy anti-cyberpunk futurism (especially in the 80s, with Britain under Thatcher's iron heel when everything looked bleak, and era which also gave rise to such wonderfully dark comics as V for Vendetta and Crisis) of a sort that made Jeff Noon's books look positively utopian (I'm sure Noon must have had stor</span>
  <span id="freeText4252734388141795659" style="display:none"><br /><br />	My memories of when I used to subscribe to the science fiction magazine Interzone in the 80s and 90s are largely of two types of stories. The magazine had a penchant for a brand of rather gloomy anti-cyberpunk futurism (especially in the 80s, with Britain under Thatcher's iron heel when everything looked bleak, and era which also gave rise to such wonderfully dark comics as V for Vendetta and Crisis) of a sort that made Jeff Noon's books look positively utopian (I'm sure Noon must have had stories in IZ, come to think of it, but I can't remember any). The second sort were dazzlingly high-concept explorations of the interface between technology and society, and where ever hastening scientific and technological progress might be taking us as a species.<br /><br />	This is where I first came into contact with Australian author Greg Egan, an Interzone regular and prime purveyor of this latter type of story. Egan's 1997 novel Diaspora is a superb example of his work. It starts toward the end of the 30th century when humanity has split into different strains – as software entities living rapid yet immortal lives in virtual reality, or interacting with the physical world inhabiting robotic bodies, or a few 'fleshers', humans who doggedly remain attached to their biological reality. An unforeseen astrophysical disaster causes some of the digital personalities to send out copies of themselves to explore the universe in search of somewhere safe from potential annihilation from cosmic accidents.<br /><br />	This is not just an updating of Stapledon's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2749148.Last_and_First_Men" title="Last and First Men by Olaf Stapledon" rel="nofollow noopener">Last and First Men</a> or Wells' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2493.The_Time_Machine" title="The Time Machine by H.G. Wells" rel="nofollow noopener">The Time Machine</a> to the information age, where biological evolution continues seamlessly into electronic, but an exploration of what it means to be human, to be conscious, to be intelligent. Is the only way to be human to remain in direct contact with the physical world and live a life measured in decades, or can a piece of software that is at least as complex and possessed of its own drives and personality and autonomy, that wants to survive and learn and has morals and ethics be also considered human? As the environments in which humans live are artificial anyway, is living in an entirely virtual world any less valid?<br /><br />	Along with a story that presents these issues, Egan takes us into areas of multi-dimensional maths and wormhole physics that stretch the readers' minds just as much, all told with a clarity and skill that makes Egan one of the finest and most important writers working in SF today.<br /><br />	Read this if you like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/545.Neal_Stephenson" title="Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Neal Stephenson</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8794.Charles_Stross" title="Charles Stross" rel="nofollow noopener">Charles Stross</a>. Other books on a similar theme include Stross' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2278387.Saturn_s_Children" title="Saturn's Children by Charles Stross" rel="nofollow noopener">Saturn's Children</a> and the wonderful <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/735504.Natural_History" title="Natural History by Justina Robson" rel="nofollow noopener">Natural History</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/224518.Justina_Robson" title="Justina Robson" rel="nofollow noopener">Justina Robson</a> (both of whom are from Leeds, which is an interesting coincidence).</span>
  <a data-text-id="4252734388141795659" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2896053904" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2896053904" />
    <a title="Doug" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38333052-doug"><img alt="Doug" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1561866244p2/38333052.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2896053904?book_show_action=true">Nov 04, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Doug" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Doug" href="/user/show/38333052-doug">Doug</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780752809250" href="/book/show/1281535.Diaspora">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2896053904" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13893439736276483854">Maybe I'm just too mathematically and scientifically challenged, but I just couldn't get into this one, though I had high hopes based on the reviews.<br/><br/>Somewhere in Egan's verbose and detailed scientific musings is a rather bland and boring story with flat characters and a dull plot. <br/><br/>If you love hard sci-fi and mathematics or quantum physics, then you'll probably love this book, I don't doubt it. <br/><br/>I just couldn't enjoy the plot and characters while having to make my way through sentences like this o</span>
  <span id="freeText13893439736276483854" style="display:none">Maybe I'm just too mathematically and scientifically challenged, but I just couldn't get into this one, though I had high hopes based on the reviews.<br /><br />Somewhere in Egan's verbose and detailed scientific musings is a rather bland and boring story with flat characters and a dull plot. <br /><br />If you love hard sci-fi and mathematics or quantum physics, then you'll probably love this book, I don't doubt it. <br /><br />I just couldn't enjoy the plot and characters while having to make my way through sentences like this one every page:<br /><br /><i>This solution has positive mass. In fact, if GR held true at this scale, it would just be a pair of black holes sharing a singularity. Of course, even for the heaviest elementary particles the Schwarzschild radius is far smaller than the Planck-Wheeler length, so quantum uncertainty would disrupt any potential event horizons, and perhaps even smooth away the singularity as well. But I wanted to find a simple, geometrical model underlying that uncertainty.</i><br /><br />It’s probably a brilliant work from a purely hard sci-fi and scientific perspective, but from a literary perspective, it fell completely flat for me. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13893439736276483854" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
